## Characteristics of studies

### Characteristics of included studies \[ordered by study ID\]

#### .

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: Iceland<br><strong>Setting</strong>: ambulatory<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults; specific ages not given<br><strong>Sample size</strong>: 54<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 34 women, 20 men<br><strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: clinical diagnosis of ADHD; and stable on prescribed ADHD medication for at least a month<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: severe mental illness; active drug abuse; verbal intelligence quotient (IQ) estimated from clinical records to be below 85; and no valid ADHD diagnosis or not prescribed/taking ADHD medication</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Intervention</strong>: CBT (15 sessions total, twice weekly, each lasting 90 minutes) + pharmacotherapy (n = 27)<br><strong>Control</strong>: treatment as usual (n = 27)<br>Methylphenidate dosages ranged between 18‐180 mg, with a mean dosage of 60.5 mg at baseline. By the end of treatment, the dosage range was 36‐162 mg, with a mean dosage of 62.5 mg.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Outcomes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Primary outcomes</strong><br><ol><li><p>ADHD symptoms:</p><ol><li><p>Kiddie‐SADS‐Present and Lifetime Version (K‐SADS‐PL)</p></li><li><p>Barkley ADHD Current Symptoms Scale (BCS‐Total Score)</p></li><li><p>Barkley ADHD Current Symptoms Scale (BCS‐Inattention)</p></li><li><p>Barkley ADHD Current Symptoms Scale (BCS‐Hyperactivity/Impulsivity)</p></li></ol></li></ol><br><strong>Secondary outcomes</strong><br><ol><li><p>Functioning ‐ Clinical Global Impression Scale‐NIMH (CGI)</p></li><li><p>Depression ‐ Beck Depression Inventory (BDI)</p></li><li><p>Anxiety ‐ Beck Anxiety Inventory (BAI)</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Notes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">We contacted authors to get the information about random sequence generation and allocation concealment that we included in this table (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0144">Young 2014 [pers comm]</a>).<br><strong>Study start date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Study end date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Funding source</strong>: Support for the study was received from research grants awarded by RANNIS the Icelandic Centre for Research (Nr. 080443022), the Landspitali Science Fund, and Janssen‐Cilag, Iceland.<br><strong>Declarations of interest</strong>: Brynjar Emilsson, Jon F Sigurdsson, Gisli Baldursson, Emil Einarsson and Halldora Olafsdottir declare that they have no competing interests. Susan Young has been a consultant for Janssen‐Cilag, Eli‐Lilly and Shire. She has given educational talks at meetings sponsored by Janssen‐Cilag, Shire, Novatis, Eli‐Lilly and Flynn‐Pharma and has received research grants from Janssen‐Cilag, Eli‐Lilly and Shire. Susan Young is a consultant for the Cognitive Centre of Canada and is co‐author of 'R&amp;R2 for ADHD Youths and Adults'. Gisli Baldursson has been a consultant for Eli‐Lilly and given educational talks at meetings sponsored by Janssen‐Cilag and Shire.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" rowspan="1"><strong><em>Risk of bias</em></strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Bias</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Authors' judgement</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Support for judgement</strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Random sequence generation (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not described</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Allocation concealment (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not described</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of participants and personnel (performance bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: it is not possible to blind personnel in a psychosocial intervention.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "The independent evaluators were psychiatrists who were blind to the treatment condition."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Incomplete outcome data (attrition bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the proportion of dropouts was 37% in both groups.<br><strong>Quote</strong>: "Missing values were not imputed because the ANCOVA calculates outcome whilst adjusting for all baseline data. Between group effect sizes for the outcome assessments were measured using Cohen's d using unadjusted means for the dependent variables and SD pooled for unequal group sizes. Fisher's exact test was used to compare proportions of medication changes. Since this study follows an ITT protocol, statistical analysis of the outcome variables were completed for all participants regardless of medication changes."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Selective reporting (reporting bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study protocol is not available, but it is clear that the published reports include all expected outcomes, including those that were prespecified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Other bias</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "The participants in both conditions were not asked to refrain from engaging in other interventions during the study period."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Conflict of interest</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: no evidence of conflicts of interest</td></tr></tbody></table>

[Open in a new tab](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/table/CD010840-tblf-0002/)

#### .

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: USA<br><strong>Setting</strong>: ambulatory<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults (18 to 24 years old)<br><strong>Sample size</strong>: 33<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 14 women, 19 men<br><strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: currently enrolled undergraduate students; meeting criteria for ADHD in adulthood, including symptom onset by age 12 and functional impairment in multiple domains<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: current substance abuse/dependence; or active suicidal ideation, major depressive episode, and history of psychotic disorder, bipolar disorder, or pervasive developmental disorder.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Intervention</strong>: dialectical behaviour therapy (DBT) (8 weekly 90 min group sessions focused on skills acquisition and strengthening, and 7 weekly 10‐15 min individual coaching phone calls focused on skills generalisation) (n = 17; 12 participants with pharmacotherapy and 5 without pharmacotherapy)<br><strong>Control</strong>: skills handouts control condition (34 pages of skills handouts, drawn from a manual for treatment of adults with ADHD and designed to reflect publicly available self‐help materials for ADHD) (n = 16; 13 participants with pharmacotherapy and 3 without pharmacotherapy)<br>Dosage, timing of dosage and administration of pharmacotherapy were not specified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Outcomes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Primary outcomes</strong><br><ol><li><p>ADHD symptoms</p><ol><li><p>Barkley Adult ADHD Rating Scale–IV (BAARS‐IV)</p></li><li><p>Brown Attention Deficit Disorder Scale – Adult Version (BADDS)</p></li></ol></li><li><p>Quality of life ‐ ADHD Quality of Life Questionnaire (AAQoL)</p></li><li><p>Depression ‐ Beck Depression Inventory, second edition (BDI‐II)</p></li><li><p>Anxiety ‐ Beck Anxiety Inventory (BAI)</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Notes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">We contacted authors to get more information, but they had not responded at the time of writing.<br><strong>Study start date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Study end date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Funding source</strong>: University of Washington – Robert C Bolles Doctoral Research Fellowship<br><strong>Declarations of interest</strong>: the author(s) declared no potential conflicts of interest with respect to the research, authorship, and/or publication of this article.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" rowspan="1"><strong><em>Risk of bias</em></strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Bias</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Authors' judgement</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Support for judgement</strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Random sequence generation (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not described</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Allocation concealment (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not described</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of participants and personnel (performance bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: it is not possible to blinding personnel in a psychosocial intervention.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: participants were assessed by an interviewer who was blind to participant condition.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Incomplete outcome data (attrition bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "The intent‐to‐treat sample included 17 and 16 participants in the DBT group skills training and self‐guided SH, respectively. One participant dropped out of DBT after four sessions and did not complete the post‐treatment or follow‐up assessments; all other participants completed treatment and the three study assessments. Missing data from this participant were imputed conservatively using the last observation carried forward (LOCF) method... Two participants receiving DBT and one receiving SH had substantial ADHD medication changes during the study (&gt; 25% change in dose or change in medication type). One participant in each treatment condition met four (rather than five) ADHD inattentive symptom criteria. All analyses were conducted with and without medication changes, and with and without participants who did not meet full DSM‐V criteria. The pattern of results did not differ; thus, results from the full intent‐to‐treat sample are reported."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Selective reporting (reporting bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study protocol is not available, but it is clear that the published reports include all expected outcomes, including those that were prespecified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Other bias</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "This study cannot rule out therapist effects or non‐specific factors of group psychotherapy, although the latter concern is mitigated by the fact that response rates with SH approximate those of supportive group psychotherapy or similar control conditions in previous trials for adults with ADHD."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Conflict of interest</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "The author(s) declared no potential conflicts of interest with respect to the research, authorship, and/or publication of this article."</td></tr></tbody></table>

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: the Netherlands<br><strong>Setting</strong>: ambulatory<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults, 18‐65 years old<br><strong>Sample size</strong>: 103<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 56 women, 47 men<br><strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: meeting criteria for ADHD in adulthood with all subtypes<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: substance abuse/ dependence within the last 6 months; comorbid psychotic disorders; borderline‐ and/or antisocial personality disorders; learning difficulties; chronic suicidal ideation; and automutilation.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Intervention</strong>: mindfulness‐based cognitive therapy (12 sessions) (n = 55; Kan has also been a member of the advisory board and consultancy team of Eli Lilly BV and was a speaker at the Adult‐ADHD Academy of Eli Lilly. The other authors declared that they have no potential conflicts of interest with respect to the research, authorship, and/or publication of this article.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" rowspan="1"><strong><em>Risk of bias</em></strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Bias</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Authors' judgement</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Support for judgement</strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Random sequence generation (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: in this study, randomisation was done by shuffling cards on which an identifier number for each participant was written.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Allocation concealment (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: an independent researcher randomly assigned the risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: it is not possible to blind personnel in a psychosocial intervention.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "The clinical interviews were conducted single blindly by a psychiatrist."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Incomplete outcome data (attrition bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the proportion of dropouts was 25% for the mindfulness‐based cognitive therapy and 12% for the waiting list group.<br><strong>Quote</strong>: "To provide a more conservative estimate of the treatment effect, the authors performed ITT analyses with imputation of missing data according to LOCF. In addition, the smaller sample size at the end of the study might have led to Type II errors, that is, not establishing differences that were present, due to insufficient power."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Selective reporting (reporting bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study protocol is not available but it is clear that the published reports include all expected outcomes, including those that were prespecified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Other bias</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: there was no evidence of other bias.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Conflict of interest</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "The author(s) declared the following potential conflicts of interest with respect to the research, authorship, and/or publication of this article: Cornelis C Kan has also been a member of the advisory board and consultancy team of Eli Lilly BV and was a speaker at the Adult‐ADHD Academy of Eli Lilly. The other authors declared that they have no potential conflicts of interest with respect to the research, authorship, and/or publication of this article."</td></tr></tbody></table>

#### .

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: Sweden<br><strong>Setting</strong>: hospital (outpatients)<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults, 18 years old or older<br><strong>Sample size</strong>: 51<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 32 women, 19 men<br><strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: ADHD as the main neurodevelopmental diagnosis; if on any psychoactive drug treatment (for ADHD or other diagnoses), the treatment should have been stable for at least three months<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: ongoing substance abuse (during the last 3 months); mental retardation (Intelligence Quotient 70); organic brain injury; autism spectrum disorder; suicidal ideation; with clinically unstable psychosocial circumstances or psychiatric disorders that were of such a severity that participation was impossible, such as being homeless, or having severe depression, psychosis, or bipolar syndrome not under stable pharmacological treatment (judged by a clinical psychologist and a psychiatrist).</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Intervention</strong>: dialectical behaviour therapy (DBT) (14 structured sessions) (n = 26; 5 participants with pharmacotherapy and 11 without pharmacotherapy)<br><strong>Control</strong>: structured discussion group (14 sessions of loosely structured discussion group) (n = 25; 14 participants with pharmacotherapy and 11 without pharmacotherapy)<br>Dosage, timing of dosage and administration of pharmacotherapy were not specified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Outcomes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Primary outcome</strong><br><ol><li><p>ADHD symptoms ‐ Current ADHD Symptoms Scale (Self Report Form)</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Notes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">We contacted authors to get more information, but they had not responded at the time of writing.<br><strong>Study start date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Study end date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Funding source</strong>: the clinical part of the study was conducted as part of the clinical work at Neuropsychiatric Unit Karolinska, Psychiatry Northwest, Stockholm County Council. The scientific parts of the projects were supported by the foundations Psykiatrifonden and Bror Gadelius Minnesfond. The funding sources had no role in study design, in the collection, analysis or interpretation of data, in the writing of the report, or in the decision to submit the paper for publication.<br><strong>Declarations of interest</strong>: all authors declare that they have no conflicts of interest related to this work.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" rowspan="1"><strong><em>Risk of bias</em></strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Bias</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Authors' judgement</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Support for judgement</strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Random sequence generation (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not described</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Allocation concealment (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not described</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of participants and personnel (performance bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: it is not possible to blind personnel in a psychosocial intervention.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study authors did not describe this aspect.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Incomplete outcome data (attrition bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the proportion of dropout was 19% for the DBT group and 20% for the structured discussion group. The study authors executed an ITT analysis.<br><strong>Quote</strong>: "Although the study plan described on treatment analysis, i.e. analysis of those that completed the treatment staying stable on medication (if they had any), we also wanted to a posteriori explore whether the results would change if those cases who did not fulfill these criteria were included in the analyses (Intention To Treat, ITT, analyses with LOCF, last observation carried forward)." In general well‐being, the effect was significant also in the ITT analyses (P &lt; 0.05).</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Selective reporting (reporting bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study protocol is not available but it is clear that the published reports include all expected outcomes, including those that were prespecified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Other bias</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: there was no evidence of other bias.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Conflict of interest</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: all study authors declared that they have no conflicts of interest related to this work.</td></tr></tbody></table>

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: Sweden<br><strong>Setting</strong>: ambulatory<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults over the age of 18<br><strong>Sample size</strong>: 57<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 39 women, 18 men<br><strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: confirmed or probable diagnosis of ADHD; current problems with organising daily activity and inattention defined, as 17 or more points on the ADHD Self‐Report Scale (ASRS; R. C. Kessler et al., 2005) subscale for Inattention (items 1–4 and 7–11); has access to a smart phone (android or Iphone) with Internet access; speaks, writes and reads Swedish; and cannot foresee any practical barriers to participation such as travels or medical operations.<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: high alcohol or drug use assessed by the AUDIT/DUDIT and assessment interview; somatic or psychiatric problems that are directly contraindicated or seriously hamper the implementation of the treatment (e.g. psychotic disorders).</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Intervention</strong>: CBT‐inspired Internet‐based course with support (Living Smart) (n = 29). The course consisted of 7 text modules distributed over 6 weeks. The weekly modules taught the use of an online calendar (via computer and smartphone) and applications for reminders and to‐do lists. Furthermore, additional apps were introduced that previously had been shown beneficial for adults with ADHD.<br><strong>Control</strong>: waiting list group (n = 28)<br>The authors did not explicitly state how many patients received pharmacological treatment.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Outcomes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Primary outcome</strong><br><ol><li><p>ADHD symptoms ‐ the WHO Adult ADHD Self‐Report Scale (ASRS)</p></li></ol><br><strong>Secondary outcomes</strong><br><ol><li><p>Functioning ‐ Sheehan disability scale (SDS)</p></li><li><p>Anxiety and Depression ‐ Hospital anxiety and depression scale (HADS)</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Notes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">We contacted authors to get more information, but they had not responded at the time of writing.<br><strong>Study start date</strong>: October 2012<br><strong>Study end date</strong>: March 2013<br><strong>Funding source</strong>: Karolinska Institutet<br><strong>Declarations of interest</strong>: not reported</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" rowspan="1"><strong><em>Risk of bias</em></strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Bias</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Authors' judgement</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Support for judgement</strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Random sequence generation (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not described</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Allocation concealment (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: it is not possible to blind personnel in a psychosocial intervention.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: blind evaluators also assessed improvement in organisation and inattention at post‐treatment.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Incomplete outcome data (attrition bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the proportion of dropout was 10% for the CBT‐inspired Internet‐based course with support (Living Smart) and 4% for the WL group.<br><strong>Quote</strong>: "The analyses were done according to the principles of intent‐to treat. All participants, including those who ended the course prematurely, were asked to fill out the post‐measurement after the 6‐week period of the online course. For all statistical analyses, observed data were used in the primary analyses. To evaluate the effect of missing data, additional sensitivity analyses were performed using last‐observation‐carried forward where the last ASRS‐score of the weekly measures was used to replace missing data at post‐treatment."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Selective reporting (reporting bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study protocol is not available, but it is clear that the published reports include all expected outcomes, including those that were prespecified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Other bias</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the lack of confirmed ADHD‐diagnoses for some of the participants and the fact that 12% did not receive an ADHD diagnose after their previous neuropsychiatric assessment and therefore were classified as sub‐clinical ADHD</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Conflict of interest</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: no evidence of conflicts of interest</td></tr></tbody></table>

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: Sweden<br><strong>Setting</strong>: hospital (outpatients)<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults (specific ages not given)  <strong>Sample size</strong>: 45<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 29 women, 16 men  <strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: having ADHD as the primary diagnosis; having access to a computer and the Internet; and being able to set aside one afternoon a week for group meetings<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: diagnosis of borderline or antisocial personality disorder and bipolar disorder; 7 participants with specific pharmacotherapy for ADHD and 6 without specific pharmacotherapy)</p></li><li><p>iCBT with weekly group‐therapy sessions (3 h once a week for 10 weeks) (n = 14; 6 participants with specific pharmacotherapy for ADHD and 8 without specific pharmacotherapy)</p></li></ol><br>Both the iCBT‐G and iCBT‐S groups followed the iCBT programme In Focus, developed by the Swedish company Livanda – Internet Clinic, Ltd., in collaboration with the NPC.<br><strong>Control</strong>: waiting list group (n = 18; 9 participants with specific pharmacotherapy for ADHD and 9 without specific pharmacotherapy)<br>Dosage, timing of dosage and administration of pharmacotherapy were not specified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Outcomes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Primary outcome</strong><br><ol><li><p>ADHD symptoms ‐ Current ADHD Symptoms Scale (self‐report)</p></li></ol><br><strong>Secondary outcomes</strong><br><ol><li><p>Depression ‐ Beck Depression Inventory, second edition (BDI‐II)</p></li><li><p>Anxiety ‐ Beck Anxiety Inventory (BAI)</p></li><li><p>Quality of life ‐ ADHD Impact Module‐Adult (AIM‐A)</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Notes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">We could not contact the authors due to incorrect email address.<br><strong>Study start date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Study end date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Funding source</strong>: the author(s) disclosed receipt of financial support for the research, authorship, and/or publication of this article: this study was financed by the 'Sjukskrivningsmiljarden', an economic fund established by the Swedish government to encourage Swedish county councils to give higher healthcare priority to sick leave and to develop processes and methods to reduce its frequency. In addition, Kent W Nilsson, as the principal investigator, received research grants from Forskningsrådet för samhällsvetenskap och arbetsliv (FAS), Systembolagets råd för alkoholforskning (SRA), the Swedish Brain Foundation, the Uppsala and Örebro Regional Research Council, Fredrik and Ingrid Thurings Foundation, the County Council of Västmanland, the König‐Söderströmska Foundation, the Swedish Psychiatric Foundation, and Svenska Spel Research Foundation. None of these organisations had a role in the study design, data collection, data analysis, data interpretation, or writing of the report.<br><strong>Declarations of interest</strong>: the author(s) declared the following potential conflicts of interest with respect to the research, authorship, and/or publication of this article: Richard Pettersson is a partner and shareholder in the company Livanda – Internet Clinic, Ltd, that constructed and owns the rights to the Internet‐based treatment programme In Focus. Richard Pettersson was also involved in the design and construction of the programme.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" rowspan="1"><strong><em>Risk of bias</em></strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Bias</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Authors' judgement</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Support for judgement</strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Random sequence generation (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "A series of 54 patients were randomised in blocks to one of the study conditions over a period of four semesters (spring 2009 to autumn 2010). The results were kept in sealed envelopes, each coupled to the number in the consecutive series of patients referred to the study and who met the inclusion criteria. Unfortunately, the study had to be adjusted before the planned sample of 54 patients had been recruited because of the referral of fewer patients than expected, as well as limited financial resources and access to personnel. A total of 45 patients had been randomised to the study at the time of adjustment."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Allocation concealment (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the randomisation protocol was created by an independent statistician.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of participants and personnel (performance bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: it is not possible to blind personnel in a psychosocial intervention.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "Independent evaluators, blinded to group assignment, administered the self‐report measures and conducted the semi‐structured interview."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Incomplete outcome data (attrition bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "The treatment dropout rate, defined as patients who did not complete all nine treatment modules, was 50% (seven patients) in the iCBT‐G group and 46% (six patients) in the iCBT‐S group. This gave a total dropout rate of 50% in the iCBT‐G group and 77% in the iCBT‐S group."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Selective reporting (reporting bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study protocol is not available, but it is clear that the published reports include all expected outcomes, including those that were prespecified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Other bias</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: there was no evidence of other bias.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Conflict of interest</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the principal author was a partner and shareholder in the company Livanda – Internet Clinic, Ltd, that constructed and owns the rights to the Internet‐based treatment programme, In Focus. <table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: USA<br><strong>Setting</strong>: ambulatory<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults (18‐65 years old)<br><strong>Sample size</strong>: 31<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 17 women, 14 men<br><strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: have had a principal diagnosis of Attention‐Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder with external validation of childhood onset and clinical severity of at least a moderate level (Clinical Global Impression; CGI of 4 or above); have been able to give informed consent and comply with study procedures; and have been stabilised on medications for ADHD or related symptoms. Stabilisation on medications was defined as no more than 10% change in medication dose over a 2‐month period with clinical evidence of improvement compared to the patients' unmedicated status.<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: moderate to severe major depression; clinically significant panic disorder; organic mental disorders, psychotic spectrum disorders, bipolar disorders, Dr Surman reported receiving research support from Abbott, Alza, Cephalon, Eli Lilly, El Minda the Hildaand Preston Davis Foundation, McNeil, Merck, New River, National Institutes of Health, Organon, Pfizer, Shire, and Takeda; being a speaker for Janssen‐Ortho, McNeil, Novartis, Shire, and MGH Academy/Reed Medical Education (which receives funding from multiple pharmaceutical companies); and being a consultant or advisor for McNeil, Shire, and Takeda. <table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" difficulties</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Intervention</strong>: mindfulness‐based cognitive therapy (12 weekly sessions) (n = 24; 15 #### .

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: USA<br><strong>Setting</strong>: ambulatory<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults (18‐65 years old)  <strong>Sample size</strong>: 88<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 58 women, 30 men  <strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: DSM‐IV diagnosis of ADHD, predominantly inattentive or combined subtype; stabilised on a given drug for at least 2 months and on a given dose for at least 1 month<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: active substance abuse or dependence; suicidal ideation; overtly hostile or aggressive behaviour likely to alienate group members; 'asocial' characteristics (e.g. pervasive developmental disorder); cognitive disability (estimated intelligence quotient (IQ) &lt; 80); psychosis; borderline personality disorder; Alzheimer's disease or other dementia; overt neurological disorder; and childhood history of abuse or trauma or other severe psychiatric condition that confounded ascertainment of childhood ADHD symptoms.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Intervention</strong>: meta‐cognitive therapy (12‐week manualised meta‐cognitive therapy group intervention; 2‐h sessions) (n = 45; 19 participants with pharmacotherapy and 26 without pharmacotherapy)<br><strong>Control</strong>: supportive therapy (12 weeks; 2‐h sessions) (n = 43; 20 participants with pharmacotherapy and 23 without pharmacotherapy)<br>Dosage, timing of dosage and administration of pharmacotherapy were not specified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Outcomes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Primary outcome</strong><br><ol><li><p>ADHD symptoms:</p><ol><li><p>Conners Adult ADHD Rating Scales–Observer: Long Version, inattention/memory subscale (T‐score)</p></li><li><p>Brown Attention‐Deficit Disorder Scale, total score (T‐score)</p></li><li><p>Adult ADHD Investigator Symptom Rating Scale Inattention subscale (AISRS)</p></li></ol></li></ol><br><strong>Secondary outcomes</strong><br><ol><li><p>Depression ‐ Beck Depression Inventory (BDI)</p></li><li><p>Anxiety ‐ Hamilton Anxiety Scale (HAM‐A)</p></li><li><p>Self‐Esteem ‐ Rosenberg Self‐Esteem Inventory</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Notes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">We contacted authors to get the information about random sequence generation and allocation concealment that we included in this table (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0124">Solanto 2014 [pers comm]</a>)<br><strong>Study start date</strong>: May 2005<br><strong>Study end date</strong>: October 2008<br><strong>Funding source</strong>: NIMH grant 1R34MH071721 to Dr Solanto<br><strong>Declarations of interest</strong>: Dr Solanto has served on the medical advisory board of Shire Pharmaceuticals and has served as a consultant and speaker for Ortho‐McNeil‐Janssen Pharmaceuticals. Dr Abikoff has received research funding from NIMH, the Hughes, Lemberg, and Heckscher Foundations, Ortho‐McNeil, Shire, and Eli Lilly, has served as a consultant to Shire, Eli Lilly, Cephalon, and Novartis, and has a financial interest in the Children's Organizational Skills Scale, published by Multi‐Health Systems. Dr Alvir is an employee of Pfizer. Drs Marks, Wasserstein, Mitchell, and Kofman report no financial relationships with commercial interests.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" rowspan="1"><strong><em>Risk of bias</em></strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Bias</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Authors' judgement</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Support for judgement</strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Random sequence generation (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: a random number sequence was electronically generated.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Allocation concealment (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>:participants were stratified by whether or not they were currently receiving medication treatment for ADHD, and otherwise randomly assigned to either the CBT or the support group.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of participants and personnel (performance bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: although individuals were, of course, not told to which group they were assigned, most were able to ultimately discern this because of the very different nature of the intervention (i.e. the participants were savvy enough to know that formal CBT is much more structured than a supportive intervention). Also, it is not possible to maintain a blinding of personnel in a psychosocial intervention.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: response was assessed via a structured interview completed by an independent (blind) evaluator, and by questionnaires completed by the patient and a significant other, immediately pre‐ and post‐treatment.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Incomplete outcome data (attrition bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "The proportion of drop‐out was 11% for Meta‐cognitive therapy and 12% for Supportive Therapy. All data were analysed both with and without non‐completers and medication changers." "The pattern of treatment contrasts indicated that the larger the score at baseline (that is, the more severe the symptoms), the greater the differential improvement observed with meta‐cognitive therapy; this occurred whether the data were analyzed with or without those who did not complete the program and those who made proscribed medication changes (interaction coefficients, 0.66 and 0.72, respectively)."</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Selective reporting (reporting bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study protocol is not available, but it is clear that the published reports include all expected outcomes, including those that were prespecified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Other bias</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: there was no evidence of other bias.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Conflict of interest</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Quote</strong>: "Dr. Solanto is currently on the Medical Advisory Board for Shire Pharmaceuticals. She has previously served as a consultant and speaker for Ortho‐McNeil‐Janssen Pharmaceuticals, Inc. During the past five years, Dr. Abikoff has received research funding from the National Institute of Mental Health, the Hughes, Lemberg and Heckscher Foundations, Ortho‐McNeil, Shire, and Eli Lilly; has consulted to Shire, Eli Lilly, Cephalon, and Novartis; and has a financial interest in the Childrens Organizational Skills Scale, published by Multi‐Health Systems. Drs. Marks, Wasserstein, Mitchell, Alvir, and Kofman have no competing interests."</td></tr></tbody></table>

#### .

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Country</strong>: Finland<br><strong>Setting</strong>: ambulatory<br><strong>Age</strong>: adults (18–49 years old)<br><strong>Sample size</strong>: 29<br><strong>Sex</strong>: 15 women, 14 men<br><strong>Inclusion criteria</strong>: ADHD diagnosis made by a physician; no diagnosis of psychosis, severe depression or paranoia; deficits of attention, executive functions or working memory identified in an earlier neuropsychological evaluation; no current alcohol dependency or drug use; not receiving a disability pension; no participation in our previous group rehabilitation study; currently not undergoing any other psychological rehabilitation; no medication or medication that has been stable for at least three months<br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong>: no neuropsychological examination; diagnosis of psychosis, severe depression or paranoia; older age, retired, or current psychological rehabilitation.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><strong>Intervention</strong>:<br><ol><li><p>Short‐term individual cognitive‐behavioral therapy (CBT) (10 weekly sessions) (n = 10; 5 participants with specific pharmacotherapy for ADHD and 5 without specific pharmacotherapy).</p></li><li><p>Cognitive training (CT) (20 sessions taking place twice a week) (n = 9; 5 participants with specific pharmacotherapy for ADHD and 4 without specific pharmacotherapy)</p></li></ol><br><strong>Control</strong>: control group (not specified) (n = 10; 7 participants with specific pharmacotherapy for ADHD and 3 without specific pharmacotherapy).<br>Dosage, timing of dosage and administration of pharmacotherapy were not specified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Outcomes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1"><ol><li><p>Brown Attention Deficit Disorder Scale – Adult Version (BADDS)</p></li><li><p>Clinical Global Impression Scale‐NIMH (CGI)</p></li><li><p>Beck Depression Inventory, second edition (BDI‐II)</p></li><li><p>Quality of Life Enjoyment and Satisfaction Questionnaire (QLESQ)</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Notes</td><td colspan="2" rowspan="1">We contacted authors to get the information about random sequence generation and allocation concealment that we included in this table (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0134">Virta 2014 [pers comm]</a>).<br><strong>Study start date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Study end date</strong>: not specified<br><strong>Funding source</strong>: this study was supported by RAY, Finland's Slot Machine Association. Maarit Virta received funding for preparation of this manuscript from the Rinnekoti Research Foundation.<br><strong>Declarations of interest</strong>: the first author report no conflicts of interest in this work.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" rowspan="1"><strong><em>Risk of bias</em></strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Bias</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Authors' judgement</strong></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Support for judgement</strong></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Random sequence generation (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the randomisation was done before the study started by raffling/draw lots. The authors had a randomised list of the rehabilitation methods beforehand. They had 4 groups: 1. CBT; 2. computerised training; 3. hypnotherapy; and 4. control. So the list looked like: 2, 1, 1, 3, 4, 1, 4, etc. Then every enrolled participant was assigned to the next group.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Allocation concealment (selection bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Unclear risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: not described</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of participants and personnel (performance bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">High risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: it is not possible to blind personnel in a psychosocial intervention.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the independent evaluator was a clinical psychologist who was blind to the actual study group of participants.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Incomplete outcome data (attrition bias)   All outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: no dropouts</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Selective reporting (reporting bias)</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study protocol is not available, but it is clear that the published reports include all expected outcomes, including those that were prespecified.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Other bias</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: there was no evidence of other bias.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Conflict of interest</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Low risk</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Comment</strong>: the study authors report no conflicts of interest in this work.</td></tr></tbody></table>

[Open in a new tab](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/table/CD010840-tblf-0015/)

**ADHD**: attention deficit hyperactivity disorder; **ANCOVA**: analysis of covariance; **ASRS**: Adult ADHD Self‐Report Scale; **CBT**: cognitive‐behavioural therapy; **CGI‐S**: Clinical Global Impressions ‐ Severity; **DBT**: dialectical behaviour therapy; **DSM**: *Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders*; **ITT**: intention‐to‐treat; **LOCF**: last observation carried forward; **MBCT**: mindfulness‐based cognitive therapy; **SD**: standard deviation; **SH**: skills handout; **WL**: waiting list.

### Characteristics of excluded studies \[ordered by study ID\]

<table><colgroup><col> <col></colgroup><thead><tr><th colspan="1" rowspan="1">Study</th><th colspan="1" rowspan="1">Reason for exclusion</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0015">Cherkasova 2016</a></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">The comparison between CBT alone versus CBT combined with medication was not a type of comparison prespecified in our protocol (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0147">Lopez 2013</a>).</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0016">Mitchell 2013</a></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">The goal of the current study was to assess the preliminary efficacy of mindfulness, without introducing other treatment modalities such as CBT.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0017">Philipsen 2015</a></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">This study compared group psychotherapy versus individual psychotherapy, affecting the comparability of the intervention of interest for our review.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0018">Serra‐Pla 2017</a></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">The goal of the current study was to assess the efficacy of virtual reality and mindfulness, without introducing other treatment modalities such as CBT.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0019">Weiss 2012</a></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">The comparison between CBT plus dextroamphetamine versus CBT plus placebo was not a type of comparison prespecified in our protocol (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0147">Lopez 2013</a>)</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0020">Young 2015</a></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">The comparison between medication plus TAU versus medication plus CBT was not a type of comparison prespecified in our protocol (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0147">Lopez 2013</a>), due to the fact that TAU was defined by the study authors as receiving usual treatment, which included both pharmacological and non‐pharmacological treatments</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0021">Young 2017</a></td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">The comparison between medication plus TAU versus medication plus CBT was not a type of comparison prespecified in our protocol (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0147">Lopez 2013</a>), due to the fact that TAU was defined by the study authors as receiving usual treatment, which included both pharmacological and non‐pharmacological treatments</td></tr></tbody></table>

**CBT**: cognitive‐behavioural therapy; **TAU**: treatment as usual.

<table><colgroup><col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Trial name or title</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">A randomized controlled study of cognitive behavioral therapy for adults with attention deficit disorder</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Methods</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Randomised controlled trial, parallel assignment</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Participants</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">108 participants<br><strong>Inclusion criteria</strong><br><ol><li><p>Outpatients from Peking University Sixth Hospital</p></li><li><p>Diagnosis of adult ADHD based on DSM‐IV</p></li><li><p>Stable on medications for adult ADHD for at least 2 months</p></li></ol><br><strong>Exclusion criteria</strong><br><ol><li><p>Severe major depression, clinically significant panic disorder, bipolar disorder, organic mental disorders, psychotic disorders, or pervasive developmental disorders</p></li><li><p>IQ less than 90</p></li><li><p>Suicide risk</p></li><li><p>Unstable physical condition</p></li><li><p>Prior participation in cognitive behavioral therapy for ADHD or other psychological therapy</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Interventions</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">CBT vs CBT plus booster sessions</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Outcomes</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Primary outcome measures</strong><br><ol><li><p>Change in ADHD Rating Scale. The Self‐Rating Anxiety Scale (SAS) with 20 items measures the level of anxiety.</p></li><li><p>Change in Self‐rating depression scale (SDS). The Self‐rating Depression Scale (SDS) is used to measure the level of depression.</p></li><li><p>Change in Behavior Rating Inventory of Executive Function‐Adult Version (BRIEF‐A). The BRIEF‐A measures the impairment level of executive function in ADHD adults.</p></li><li><p>Change in Cambridge Neuropsychological Test Automatic Battery (CANTAB). The CANTAB is a computerised neuropsychological test measuring the cognitive and executive function.</p></li><li><p>Change in Barratt impulsiveness scale (BIS). The Barratt impulsiveness scale including 30 items is used to measure impulsiveness.</p></li><li><p>Change in self‐esteem scale (SES). The self‐esteem scale measures the level of self‐esteem.</p></li><li><p>Change in WHO Quality of Life‐Brief Version (WHOQOL‐BREF). The WHOQOL‐BREF is the short version of WHO Quality of Life scale and includes 26 items measuring the level of life quality.</p></li><li><p>Change in Brain Oxygenation Level Dependent (BOLD) Signal. The level of BOLD activity during working memory task after CBT is compared with baseline level to explore the potential effects of CBT on brain.</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Starting date</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Current Controlled Trials: <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT02062411">NCT02062411</a>, date of registration: 12 February 2014<br><strong>Start date</strong>: October 2013<br><strong>End date</strong>: March 2016<br><strong>Recruitment status</strong>: completed</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Contact information</td><td colspan="1" rowspan="1">Dr Fang Huang.
