## Differences between protocol and review

1. [Description of the condition](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0038)
	1. We pointed out the differences between ICD‐10 and DSM‐5, and we updated the reference about the prevalence of ADHD in childhood.
2. [Why it is important to do this review](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0041)
	1. Where we argued that between 20% and 50% of people with ADHD do not respond to drug treatment, we added a sentence about the side effects of psychopharmacological treatment.
		2. When the protocol was published, no systematic review on this topic existed. At the time of publication of this review, we found three systematic reviews. We added this information.
3. [Types of interventions](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0047)
	1. We modified the comparisons that considered CBT was considered as monotherapy to clarify the different types of control groups. In the protocol ([Lopez 2013](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0147)), we considered: "monotherapy (CBT versus control (supportive psychotherapies, placebo interventions, waiting list or no treatment) and CBT versus usual treatment (other specific psychotherapies for ADHD)); and combined therapy (CBT combined with pharmacotherapy versus pharmacotherapy alone)". In the review, we redefined the comparison as follows: CBT versus unspecific control conditions (supportive psychotherapies, waiting list or no treatment); CBT plus pharmacotherapy versus pharmacotherapy alone; CBT versus other specific interventions (control interventions that include therapeutic ingredients specifically targeted to ADHD).
4. [Types of outcome measures](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0048)
	1. To clarify the analysis of the outcomes, we added the following paragraph: "We presented clinical and self‐reported outcomes separately, as do most studies about this topic, because assessing ADHD is more accurate when symptom information comes from more than one source ([Barkley 1998a](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0034))."
		2. We stated in the protocol that we planned to include studies that assessed at least one primary outcome or secondary outcome ([Lopez 2013](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0147)), but in the full review we clarified that we included studies that assessed at least one primary outcome or at least one secondary outcome.
		3. The safety outcome 'All‐cause treatment discontinuation', which we considered a secondary outcome in the protocol ([Lopez 2013](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0147)), is now considered a primary outcome in the full review.
		4. We included the criteria to assess the magnitude of effect for continuous outcomes using the suggestions in section 12.6.2 'Re‐expressing SMDs using rules of thumb for effect sizes' of the *Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions* ([Higgins 2011](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0090)): 0.2 represents a small effect, 0.5 a moderate effect, and 0.8 a large effect.
5. [Electronic searches](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0056).
	1. We planned to use MEDLINE Ovid but we used MEDLINE PubMed because of the availability of this interface.
		2. We replaced the *meta* Register of Controlled Trials (*m* RCT), which was reported as "under review", with the World Health Organization International Clinical Trials Registry Portal (WHO ICTRP).
6. [Searching other resources](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0057)
	1. We added the World Congress of Behavioral and Cognitive Therapies to search for conference abstracts.
7. [Assessment of risk of bias in included studies](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0061)
	1. We expanded the description of the process (see [Table 11](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-tbl-0011)).
8. [Dealing with missing data](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0068)
	1. If the studies did not report the standard deviation (SD), we planned to calculate it from the P value, t values, CIs or standard errors (as described in section 7.7.3.3 of the *Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions*; [Higgins 2011](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0090)). If this information was not reported or was unattainable, we planned to impute the SD from the study with the highest SD for that outcome and assess the effects of this assumption on the analysis by conducting a sensitivity analysis. If the outcome data were reported as a median, a range or as a mean without a variance, we planned to report the data in additional tables. Since these situations did not happen, we did not apply these planned approaches.
9. [Assessment of heterogeneity](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0069)
	1. We revised the bands that we reported for I <sup>2</sup> from "0% to 30%: might not be important; 30% to 60%: may represent moderate heterogeneity; more than 60%: may represent substantial or considerable heterogeneity" ([Lopez 2013](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0147)), to "0% to 40%: might not be important; 30% to 60%: may represent moderate heterogeneity; 50% to 90%: may represent substantial heterogeneity; and 75% to 100%: represents considerable heterogeneity".
10. [Data synthesis](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0071)
	1. We explained the conditions for consideration of homogeneity in more detail.
		2. We also included a new subsection named 'Summary of findings' beneath this section, to explain the criteria we used to rate the quality of evidence according to the GRADE methodology.
11. [Subgroup analysis and investigation of heterogeneity](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0072)
	1. We decided to remove 'type of CBT' as a possible subgroup analysis after the comparisons were redefined (see 'Types of interventions' above).
12. [Sensitivity analysis](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0073)
	1. As we did not include cluster‐RCTs, we did not perform sensitivity analyses to assess the potential biasing effects of inadequately controlled cluster‐RCTs ([Donner 2001](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0068)), or the effect of different values of the ICC.
		2. We did not conduct, as planned, a sensitivity analysis comparing the results of the analyses with our imputed 'highest SD' versus analyses that used an SD imputed from the study with the lowest SD.
		3. We did not conduct, as planned, a sensitivity analysis to assess the effects of eventual missing dichotomous data on our primary meta‐analyses by assuming, on the one hand, that all missing data were successes and, on the other hand, that all missing data were failures (best‐ versus worst‐case scenario analyses).
13. [Effects of interventions](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0099)
	1. While we did not foresee the transformation of the continuous results to relative percentage changes in the protocol, we included it in [Appendix 2](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0168) to facilitate the readers' understanding.

### 5\. Differences between protocol and full review.

<table><colgroup><col> <col> <col></colgroup><tbody><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Section of Review</strong></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Protocol</strong></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><strong>Full review</strong></td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0038">Description of the condition</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"The International Classification of Diseases (ICD‐10) offers a similar definition for hyperkinetic disorders (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0137">WHO 1993</a>). Along with these three main symptomatic clusters, people with ADHD also present with deficits in executive functions, behaviour and emotion regulation, and motivation (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0054">Brown 2000</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0136">Wender 2001</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0067">Davidson 2008</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0131">Torrente 2011</a>). There is a high prevalence of comorbid disorders, estimated at 50% to 75% (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0094">Kessler 2006</a>), including anxiety, depression and substance abuse (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0047">Biederman 1993</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0106">Murphy 1996</a>). Epidemiological studies estimate that the prevalence of ADHD is approximately 5% in childhood (Polanczyk 2007) and approximately 2.5% in adulthood (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0123">Simon 2009</a>)."</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"The <em>International Classification of Diseases</em> (ICD‐10) offers a similar definition for hyperkinetic disorders (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0137">WHO 1993</a>), but the required number of symptoms and the age of onset are different. Along with these three main symptomatic clusters, people with ADHD also present with deficits in executive functions, behaviour and emotion regulation, and motivation (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0054">Brown 2000</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0067">Davidson 2008</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0131">Torrente 2011</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0136">Wender 2001</a>). There is a high prevalence of comorbid disorders, estimated at 50% to 75% (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0094">Kessler 2006</a>), including anxiety, depression and substance abuse (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0047">Biederman 1993</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0106">Murphy 1996</a>). Epidemiological studies estimate that the prevalence of ADHD is approximately 5% in childhood and around 2.5% in adulthood (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0111">Polanczyk 2014</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0123">Simon 2009</a>)."</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2" align="left" colspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0041">Why it is important to do this review</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"Between 20% and 50% of people with ADHD do not respond to drug treatment (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0138">Wilens 2002</a>)."</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"Between 20% and 50% of people with ADHD do not respond to drug treatment (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0138">Wilens 2002</a>). Also, pharmacological treatment is frequently associated with relevant side effects in both children and adults (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0030">AJCD 2001</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0056">Castells 2013</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0064">Cunill 2013</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0084">Graham 2011</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0095">King 2006</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0101">Lim 2006</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0105">Morton 2000</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0108">Perrin 2008</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0112">Prescrire 2007</a>). Due to these concerns, it is important to have non‐pharmacological interventions for treating adults with ADHD."</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"To date, no systematic review has examined the effects of CBT in adults with ADHD. The growing number of randomised controlled trials assessing the efficacy of CBT for this population (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0096">Knouse 2008</a>) suggest that this review is timely."</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"To our knowledge, three systematic reviews have compared the effects of CBT in adults with ADHD (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0091">Jensen 2016</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0098">Knouse 2017</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0145">Young 2016</a>). However, there are important methodological differences between them, also with respect to our review. Both <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0091">Jensen 2016</a> and <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0145">Young 2016</a> employed more restrictive criteria for defining CBT treatments that excluded relevant CBT variants such as mindfulness‐based cognitive therapy and dialectical behavioural therapy. <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0098">Knouse 2017</a> did not report grades of quality of evidence of the included studies."</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0047">Types of interventions</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We considered the following comparisons:<br>Monotherapy<br><ol><li><p>CBT versus control (supportive psychotherapies, placebo interventions, waiting list or no treatment)</p></li><li><p>CBT versus usual treatment (other specific psychotherapies for ADHD)</p></li></ol><br>Combined therapy<br><ol><li><p>CBT combined with pharmacotherapy versus pharmacotherapy alone</p></li></ol></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We considered the following comparisons:<br><ol><li><p>"CBT versus unspecific control conditions (supportive psychotherapies, waiting list or no treatment).</p></li><li><p>CBT plus pharmacotherapy versus pharmacotherapy alone.</p></li><li><p>CBT versus other specific interventions (control interventions that include therapeutic ingredients specifically targeted to ADHD)."</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="3" align="left" colspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0048">Types of outcome measures</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We considered psychometrically validated self‐report measures or those completed by an independent rater or relative. The measures were considered short‐ (up to six months), medium‐ (six months to 12 months) and long‐term (more than 12 months)."</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"We considered psychometrically validated self‐report measures or those completed by an independent rater or relative.<br>"We presented clinical and self‐reported outcomes separately, as do most studies about this topic, because assessing ADHD is more accurate when symptom information comes from more than one source (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0034">Barkley 1998a</a>).<br>"We considered the measures as short term (up to 6 months), medium term (6 months to 12 months) and long term (more than 12 months).<br>"We included studies that assessed at least one primary outcome or at least one secondary outcome."</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"We will include studies that have assessed at least one primary or secondary outcome."</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"We included studies that assessed at least one primary outcome or at least one secondary outcome."</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">The safety outcome 'All‐cause treatment discontinuation' was considered as secondary outcome</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We considered the safety outcome 'All‐cause treatment discontinuation' to be a primary outcome.</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0056">Electronic searches</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We planned to search Ovid MEDLINE</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We used MEDLINE PubMed because of the availability of this interface.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0057">Searching other resources</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"Additionally, we searched dissertations and abstracts from the following.<br><ol><li><p>Association for Behavioural and Cognitive Therapies (ABCT)</p></li><li><p>World Congress on ADHD, organised by the World Federation of ADHD</p></li><li><p>Annual Meeting ‐ American Psychiatric Association (APA)"</p></li></ol></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"Additionally, we searched dissertations and abstracts from the following.<br><ol><li><p>Association for Behavioural and Cognitive Therapies (ABCT) Convention, 2008 to 2017 (<a href="http://www.abct.org/Conventions/?m=mConvention&amp;fa=PastFutureConvention">www.abct.org/Conventions/?m=mConvention&amp;fa=PastFutureConvention</a>).</p></li><li><p>World Congress on ADHD, organised by the World Federation of ADHD, 2007 to 2017 (<a href="http://www.xn--adhdfederation-862h.org/congresshistory">www.adhd‐federation.org/congresshistory</a>).</p></li><li><p>Annual Meeting ‐ American Psychiatric Association (APA), 1973 to 2016 (<a href="http://www.psychiatry.org/psychiatrists/search%E2%80%90directories%E2%80%90databases/library%E2%80%90and%E2%80%90archive">www.psychiatry.org/psychiatrists/search‐directories‐databases/library‐and‐archive</a>)."</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0061">Assessment of risk of bias in included studies</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"We independently evaluated the risk of bias using EROS. This process followed the six criteria described in Table 8.5.d, "Criteria for judging risk of bias in the 'Risk of bias' assessment tool" of the <em>Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions</em> (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0090">Higgins 2011</a>) (See <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-tbl-0004">Table 4</a>)."</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"We evaluated the risk of bias in each included trial using the seven criteria described in Table 8.5.d ('Criteria for judging risk of bias in the "Risk of bias' assessment tool"<em>)</em> of the <em>Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions</em> (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0090">Higgins 2011</a>). Two review authors (PL and FT) independently assessed each included study as being at low, high or unclear (uncertain) risk of bias for each domain, using EROS software (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0058">Ciapponi 2011</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0082">Glujovsky 2011</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0081">Glujovsky 2010</a>); see <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-tbl-0004">Table 4</a>. If there were discrepancies between their assessments, and the two review authors were unable to reach a consensus, a third review author (AC) joined the decision‐making process. All three review authors discussed the issue and made a final decision."</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0069">Assessment of heterogeneity</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">The bands that we reported for I <sup>2</sup> were:<br><ol><li><p>"0% to 30%: may not be important</p></li><li><p>30% to 60%: may represent moderate heterogeneity</p></li><li><p>more than 60%: may represent substantial or considerable heterogeneity"</p></li></ol></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">The bands that we reported for I <sup>2</sup> were:<br><ol><li><p>"0% to 40%: might not be important.</p></li><li><p>30% to 60%: may represent moderate heterogeneity.</p></li><li><p>50% to 90%: may represent substantial heterogeneity.</p></li><li><p>75% to 100%: considerable heterogeneity."</p></li></ol></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2" align="left" colspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0071">Data synthesis</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"When we considered studies to be sufficiently homogenous in terms of participants, interventions and outcomes, we synthesised the results in a meta‐analysis using RevMan (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0116">RevMan 2014</a>)."</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"We synthesised the results in a meta‐analysis using Review Manager 5 (RevMan 5) when we considered studies to be sufficiently homogenous in terms of population (regarding sex, age and diagnosis), interventions (comparable modalities of CBT) and comparisons (as a monotherapy or a part of a combined treatment) to avoid clinical heterogeneity, and in terms of outcome measurement methods to avoid methodological heterogeneity (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0116">RevMan 2014</a>). Two authors assessed homogeneity independently and solved discrepancies by consensus."</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We did not include a subsection about summary of findings</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We included a 'Summary of findings table' subsection:<br>"We prepared a 'Summary of findings' table for our three main comparisons (see <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0047">Types of interventions</a>) according to GRADE methodology (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0033">Atkins 2004</a>; <a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0086">Guyatt 2011</a>), using GRADEpro GDT software (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0083">GRADEpro 2015</a>). We included our primary outcome, the core symptoms of ADHD (self‐, clinician‐ or observer‐reported), in the tables.<br>"Two review authors (AC and PL) independently assessed the quality of the evidence as high, moderate, low or very low, downgrading the rating according to the presence of the following criteria: study limitations, in which we considered the studies' 'Risk of bias' level; imprecision; inconsistency of results; indirectness of evidence; and likely publication bias.<br>"To assess the magnitude of effect for continuous outcomes, we used the criteria suggested in the <em>Cochrane Handbook for Systematic Reviews of Interventions</em> (<a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-bbs2-0090">Higgins 2011</a>): 0.2 represents a small effect, 0.5 a moderate effect, and 0.8 a large effect."</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0072">Subgroup analysis and investigation of heterogeneity</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We considered the type of CBT as a possible subgroup analysis</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We did not include the type of CBT as a possible subgroup analysis after we redefined the comparisons.</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0073">Sensitivity analysis</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">For this review, we had planned to undertake sensitivity analyses to determine the effect of restricting the analysis to: "(a) only studies with low risk of selection bias (associated with sequence generation or allocation concealment), (b) only studies with low risk of performance bias (associated with issues of blinding), and (c) only studies with low risk of attrition bias (associated with completeness of data). In addition, we will assess the sensitivity of findings to any imputed data within a study."</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">"[W]e undertook sensitivity analyses to determine the effect of removing from the analysis: studies with high risk of selection bias (associated with sequence generation or allocation concealment); studies with high risk of performance bias (associated with issues of blinding); and studies with high risk of attrition bias (associated with completeness of data). In addition, we assessed the sensitivity of findings to any imputed data within a study."</td></tr><tr><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-sec-0099">Effects of interventions</a></td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">The transformation of the continuous results to relative percentage changes had not been foreseen.</td><td align="left" colspan="1" rowspan="1">We included the transformation of continuous results to relative percentage changes in Appendix 2.</td></tr></tbody></table>

[Open in a new tab](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/table/CD010840-tbl-0011/)

We describe all modifications in the Additional Methods Table ([Table 11](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6494390/#CD010840-tbl-0011)).
